Hello Everyone, welcome to CITS2002 Systems Programming in 2018.

FIRSTLY, if you read nothing else, please read this section.

Our unit this year has a record enrolment - about 320 confirmed
enrolments, up from 305 last year.  Our lecture theatre holds 335,
so it'll be a tight squeeze for a couple of weeks.  and we don't have
sufficient lab times or spaces for all students to be guaranteed a seat
in our weekly lab sessions.

(I'm not panicking about this and I don't think that you'll need to
 either.  (Too many) years of experience have shown that a reasonable
 proportion of students choose to not attend all lectures and a
 weekly lab, choosing to just watch the LCS lecture recordings and
 use their own labtops for all laboratory and project work.  In
 previous years I have worried about this, adding extra repeat
 lectures, only to find that the only available times and venues
 were at 5PM or 6PM;  barely 20 students attended the repeat sessions,
 and students drifted away from the original lectures anyway.)

-------

We'll spend part of the first lecture on Monday discussing how the unit
will run this year, but first I'll head off some common questions that
students are asking by email:

Firstly, we're not using LMS/Blackboard for this unit.
All teaching materials will be available from:

    http://teaching.csse.uwa.edu.au/units/CITS2002/

LECTURES:
    We have 2 45 minute lectures each week. All lectures will be recorded
    and available via LCS. The schedule of lecture topics, in fact all
    sessions in the unit, is available from the left-margin of the
    unit's homepage. HTML and PDFs of all lectures will be available
    to read or download ahead of time.
    Attendance at lectures is not compulsory.

WORKSHOPS:
    Most weeks we'll hold a workshop on Fridays, to review the week's
    material by focusing on the design and implementation of a short
    program addressing a single problem. You'll receive the problem
    ahead of time, hopefully sketch out a solution, and we'll discuss
    it in the Friday workshops. The workshops are in a lecture venue,
    not a computing lab, so you may wish to bring your laptops along.
    Attendance at workshops is not compulsory.

LABORATORIES:
    The allocated laboratory times are times at which there'll be someone
    there to assist you.  Most weeks, when the two projects are not
    "running", will have a laboratory sheet of 3-4 "standard" questions,
    and often a couple of more difficult questions, for you to attempt
    that week.  Sample solutions to the standard questions will be
    provided.  If you can successfully complete the sample questions,
    each week, then you're keeping up in the unit.
    Attendance at laboratories is not compulsory.  Come along if you
    need to use a computer, or if you need some help with a question.
    There's no requirement to stay the full 2 hours.  Labs are open
    24/7 although, of course, students allocated to other units' lab
    sessions have priority.

I hope that you'll enjoy CITS2002 this semester.

Chris McDonald

Date: Tue Jul 31 10:52:00 2018

Subject: main Function Parameters
From: chris.mcdonald@uwa.edu.au
X-smilie: none
X-img: none

A student sent me this by email, but it's the very sort of question that I'm 
encouraging  you all to post here so that all students can see the answer:

> Hi Chris,
> 
> I'm currently enrolled in CITS2002 for semester 2, 2018. I wanted to ask a question
> about the sample program you used in the lecture today in your discussion about
> program structure.
> 
> The main function in this program took 2 parameters - an integer argc, and a
> character array argv[] (which if I am correct, is a pointer of some sort).
> Is it too soon to ask what the purpose of these parameters are?
> Will all or most of the C programs we will be dealing with in this unit
> contain these 2 parameters?

Hi,

Never to early to ask questions about anything.  Your 'guess' is correct, two 
parameters, one an integer count of the number of command-line arguments received by 
the program  (given/passed to the program by the operating system), and the other is 
the actual parameters received (in the manner that C represents character strings).

They are usually pronounced 'arg-c' and 'arg-v',
for argument count, and argument vector.

We'll certainly be covering this is more detail later (Lab 2?), but if you're reading 
ahead in a textbook or online tutorial, the term "command line arguments" will easily 
find something.  Here's an example program demonstrating their use:


#include <stdio.h>

int main(int argc, char *argv[])
{
    printf("number of arguments received: %i\n", argc);
    for(int a=0 ; a < argc ; ++a) {
        printf("  argv[%i] = \"%s\"\n", a, argv[a]);
    }
    return 0;
}


If invoked as:    ./try passing these arguments
you'll see the output:

number of arguments received: 4
  argv[0] = "./try"
  argv[1] = "passing"
  argv[2] = "these"
  argv[3] = "arguments"

Hi Chris,

Many thanks. So if I was to see a program with the header of the main function as:

int main(void)

I would be correct to assume that this program takes no input from the operating system, but can 
still take input from a user through the use of "scanf()" statements. Is this correct?

Date: Tue Jul 31 12:01:41 2018
To: help2002@csse.uwa.edu.au
Received: from 130.95.1.70
Subject: main Function Parameters
From: chris.mcdonald@uwa.edu.au
X-smilie: none
X-img: none

ANONYMOUS wrote:

> Hi Chris,
> 
> Many thanks. So if I was to see a program with the header of the main function as:
> 
> int main(void)
> 
> I would be correct to assume that this program takes no input from the operating system, but can 
> still take input from a user through the use of "scanf()" statements. Is this correct?

Hi,

(no real need for post anonymously, here, we're all friends).

More correctly, if the parameters are main(void), then the program has no way of *accessing* its 
parameters - the operating system has no way of knowing if the program *wants/needs* its parameters, so they 
are still passed.

The program can still receive its inputs from many sources - the standard-input stream (via scan() as 
you've suggested), named files, and the process's environment variables  (perhaps more search phases to 
look up!).

Previously, <poster017@student.uwa.edu.au> wrote:

> For people who uses Windows or other operating systems, how do we open up a Terminal 
> to run the code from Vim?

Firstly you'll need to install vim on your Windows machine;  you'll find the vim package, and 
instructions and other documentation on the vim website:

  https://www.vim.org
  https://www.vim.org/download.php

and you can then receive more help from websites with the exact same question:

  https://superuser.com/questions/409486/how-do-you-run-vim-in-windows

If using Windows-10 (but not Windows-7), you can further download and install the Linux 
subsystem from which you can invoke vim  (or it may also come with its own copy of vim, or, 
vi - I'm not sure).

Maybe students far more familiar with Windows experience can offer other or better describe 
other options, too.

____
Under Linux it's easier, as a standard terminal program, named xterm, and vi/vim will already 
be installed by a standard distribution.  Running an instance of Xterm, and from inside that 
an instance of vi/vim, should be easy as a terminal menu item will have been installed on the 
desktop menu by default  (with a standard distribution).

Date: Mon Aug  6 14:30:08 2018
To: help2002@csse.uwa.edu.au
Received: from 130.95.1.70
Subject: Trying to understand the interaction with a C program
From: chris.mcdonald@uwa.edu.au
X-smilie: none
X-img: none

ANONYMOUS wrote:

> Hi Chris

Hi, though you don't have to address questions to me, here, as anyone is welcome to 
contribute.
 
> I'm a bit stuck on this. I've just came from Java and I am trying to understand 
> the interaction with a C program once it's compiled. 
> 
> Firstly, are methods the same as functions? I don't think they are but not sure.

I'm a pedant for the correct terminology; using the correct terminology quickly 
clarifies what's being discussed or asked.
C has functions, and that's it.  Some functions in C return a value, such as an integer 
or character,  some C functions don't return a value (termed 'void functions' which 
we'll discuss soon).

C does not have methods; Java has methods, which explicitly act on an object, setting 
or getting properties of that object.  No objects in C, so not methods.


 
> Secondly, is it possible to have more than one function in a text edit file that 
> performs different tasks to other functions? If so, how do you call those 
> specific functions that you want to perform the task (I might be pushing towards 
> Java here)?  If you write hundreds of calculation (eg. sum = A+B) functions in a 
> file and then perform /a.out  what function will execute? 

The C code inside the 1st labsheet file rot.c is an example of a C program that has 
multiple functions - one named main, the other named rotate.

When a program is first invoked/executed, the operating system finds the in-memory 
address of the main function, and commences execution of the instructions from that 
address.  It is not possible to commence the execution of a program from, say, the 
rotate function.  Just main.

If you wrote a program named, say, arithmetic and you wanted to use it as:

  ./arithmetic 3 + 7
  ./arithmetic 12 / 4
  ./arithmetic -7 - 10

then, because execution can only begin at main, you'd probably write your code in main 
to determine what needs to be executed (based on +, /, or -) and then main would call 
one of the program's three other functions to perform the required arithmetic.

?

Previously, <poster017@student.uwa.edu.au> wrote:

> For people who uses Windows or other operating systems, how do we open up a Terminal 
> to run the code from Vim?
> 
> **I'm not suggesting that the Macs in the labs won't be used to practice a Mac 
> system, but it is for us to do extra practice from home.
> 
> Thanks

Took me a few hours to get some idea of how to do this on Windows natively. (I heard it's significantly easier 
on the average Linux distribution due to it being Unix-like or something, which I'm looking into right now)

Step 1: Get the following software:

*A text editor of choice, I love Vim. Get it from one of the following websites:
https://www.vim.org/download.php
https://chocolatey.org/packages/vim

*A compiler of choice, MinGW seems to do the job for now. Get it from here: 
http://www.mingw.org/
https://chocolatey.org/packages/mingw

*(Optional, shameless plug, I promise I'm not just a 'shill') A package manager for Windows, like chocolatey 
(Required if you use the second of the links I provided). Available here:
https://chocolatey.org/install

Step 2: Read up on the difference between 'DOS commands' and 'Unix commands'. Learn to navigate the Windows cmd, 
which seems to be DOS-like. Useful links below:
http://www.dba-oracle.com/t_windows_dos_unix_linux_commands.htm
https://www.digitalcitizen.life/command-prompt-how-use-basic-commands

Step 3: Read the week 1 lab-sheet carefully.
http://teaching.csse.uwa.edu.au/units/CITS2002/labsheets/labsheet1.php

Step 4: Have 'fun' navigating text files without touching the mouse using Vim/gVim, the built-in vimtutor is a 
good weekend activity and relatively productive procrastination.

Step 5: Open up cmd, via the Start menu (Start button) or the Run menu (Ctrl+R). Follow the instructions as 
outlined in the week 1 lab-sheet. Replace any mention of cc with gcc, and Unix-like commands with DOS-like 
commands.
For example: 
ls → dir
rm blah rot → del blah rot
cc -std=c99 -Wall -pedantic -o rot rot-warning.c → gcc -std=c99 -Wall -pedantic -o rot rot-warning.c

Or you can give up on Windows and use Linux or OSX and follow the lab-sheet to the letter and possibly have a 
slightly easier time. Or use an IDE like Visual Studio Code.

Hopefully, the guide above helped you a bit and wasn't too overly confusing/badly written. Anyone who is more 
informed on using Windows/aforementioned tools than I am, please don't hesitate to correct/improve my answer, 
because as I mentioned before, I learnt this through 2 hours of Googling and am a fellow student who is new to 
this as well.

____________________

Alan Chen
poster101

Date: Tue Aug  7 10:24:48 2018

Thanks for your detailed response Alan; sounds like 2hrs of learning well spent, and now shared.

Just a couple of clarifications, prompted from earlier questions:

Microsoft's Visual Studio coding platform does not have full (or clear) support for C; they dropped support for C at least 5 
years ago.  Visual Studio does support C++ (though I can imagine that support will be dropped eventually as MS pushes 
further with C#).

However, C and C++ are different languages; historically C++ was spawned from C, but they now follow two distinct standards, 
and neither is a superset nor subset of the other.  Yes, you can write a program that compiles and runs successfully with 
either a C or a C++ compiler, 

This webpage appears to give a reasonable summary:
  http://cs-fundamentals.com/tech-interview/c/difference-between-c-and-cpp.php

So while you can develop C++ programs, with only C features, within Visual Studio, the quality and accuracy of the support 
will diminish with more complicated programs.

___
It's a bigger task, but the C compiler supported on macOS, named Clang, and the C compiler supported on Linux, named gcc, 
(which can each be invoked just with the command 'cc') are each supported on Windows.

But you're not been encouraged to give up on Windows, nor being forced to use a weird platform, but given the opportunity to 
learn and use one of the 'other' 3 contemporary desktop operating systems. We're all here to learn (presumably).

Hi,

Sorry Alan if this disregards all your hard work, but I think I managed to find something easier for Windows users.

Date: Sat Aug 11 11:55:05 2018
To: help2002@csse.uwa.edu.au
Received: from 124.177.207.212
Subject: Q9 Labsheet 1
From: poster026@student.uwa.edu.au
X-smilie: none
X-img: none

I'm not sure if how I did it is best but I managed it by using the code below 
and it seemed to work. Essentially it just tries to open the file and if it can 
reads and rotates the characters from the file one at a time until it reaches 
the end of file. 

FILE *myfile = fopen(argv[i], "r");

if(myfile == NULL) {
	fprintf(stderr, "%s: could not be opened", argv[i]);
}
else {
	char ch;
	while((ch = fgetc(myfile)) != EOF) {
		printf("%c", rotate(ch));
	}
}

For those interested this is the code in the expired hastebin link

//Name of function takes char parameter called filename
char rotFile(char* filename) {
//return value set to 0 (is this boolean?)
    int return_val = 1;
//FILE variable fp = opening filename and reading it/
    FILE* fp = fopen(filename, "r");
   
//If fb exists? 
    if(fp) 
    {
        //read fp
        fseek(fp, 0, SEEK_END);
        size = ftell(fp);
         char buf[size+1];
        fseek(fp, 0, SEEK_SET);
        fread(buf,1,size,fp);
        fclose(fp);

        return ('a' + (buf - 'a' + ROT) % 26);
    }
    
else 
    { return return_val; 
        }

Date: Tue Aug 14 10:16:28 2018
To: help2002@csse.uwa.edu.au
Received: from 10.21.62.49
Subject: Terminal tips and tricks
From: chris.mcdonald@uwa.edu.au
X-smilie: none
X-img: none

(replying to a student's email, so that all can see the answer)

> Hi Chris, I saw that you edit your code on the terminal rather than on vim and I
> want to learn how to do that. I reviewed the most recent video and saw that
> you put an alias "fg" to edit your code so what should I type to edit my code? Thanks.

Hi,

Actually, there's a number of things going on there:

Firstly, the traditional (30 year old) way to run the vim editor *is* inside the same window
that receives your shell commands.  On more modern(!) graphical displays we've been using the
mvim, or gvim, commands which simply popup a stand-alone copy of the editor in its own window.
I choose to edit files and compile them in the same window during tutorials (and in most of my
work) to simply save screen real-estate.  So you can run the vim editor in the single window by
simply executing the 'vim file.c' command at the prompt, and then leaving the editor with either

    :wq!<RETURN>
or  ZZ

when finished editing  (or just  :q!  to quit without saving changes).

The second part is more complex, and will be covered later in the unit.
Processes may be in one of a number of states - (simplified definition):

  - running in the foreground, receiving keyboard input
  - suspended, not running, receiving no input, or
  - running in the background, receiving no input.

When working (as in tutorials) I first invoke a copy of the vim editor, and it runs in the
foreground receiving input from the keyboard.  I then type 2 keys at once - Control-Z - which
suspends the vim process.  The shell notices this, and issues the next prompt, and which I can
run the compiler again (or any other commands).  When I wish to resume execution of the vim
process - shifting it from suspended to foreground - I type 'fg', the shell becomes suspended,
vim resumes, detects that it's again running in the foreground, and redraws the screen.

For this sequence to be effective, you also need the editor to write its changes to disk before
becoming suspended. Either you can do this manually, which gets tiring and you may forget, or
you can ask vim to do it automatically by setting an internal vim setting -

  :set autowrite<RETURN>

Then, just before vim is suspended it writes its changes to disk, and the next compilation, etc,
will be performed using the updated C file.

In much the same way that shell aliases can be saved between visits, vim's settings can also be
saved in a file named  ~/.vimrc   which you can edit with vim itself, adding the line:

  set autowrite     (no leading colon).

Date: Wed Aug 15 13:04:20 2018
To: help2002@csse.uwa.edu.au
Received: from 130.95.1.70
Subject: The ACM Inroads "Programs as Art" Contest
From: chris.mcdonald@uwa.edu.au
X-smilie: none
X-img: none

The “Obfuscated C Contest” is a well-known competition that challenges programmers to write obscure/obfuscated C 
programs.  Though not a requirement, many of these programs are structured in a manner that spells out words or 
images; see for example https://www.ioccc.org/2018/endoh2/prog.c  (All the winners of the 2018 contest are available 
at https://www.ioccc.org/years.html#2018 )
 
The ACM Inroads Back Page “Programs-as-Art” contest builds on the idea of code as art and has two categories:
 
1. ACM/Computing.  Programs in this category must be organized to show some word or image related to ACM or 
computing, e.g., the word “ACM”, “INROADS”, “SIGCSE”, “50” (for the 50th year of SIGCSE), images of some piece of 
hardware, etc.  See the link given above for an example of a program as an image.  The program must also compile, run 
and print out text relating to ACM.  For example, it could print the names of all Turing Award winners or the names 
of all the ACM SIGs, etc.  The output may, but does not have to be, an image.
 
2. Open.  Programs in this category may be organized to show any word or image.  The program must also compile, run 
and print out text relating to word/image of the program.  The output may, but does not have to be, an image.

The best entries in each category (judged by the Inroads editorial staff) will appear in a future issue of Inroads 
magazine on the Back Page.
 
________
Rules:
 
1. You must use one of the following languages:  Java, C, C++, C#. If you would like to use a different language that 
has an open-source compiler or interpreter, contact John at barr@ithaca.edu.

2. Your program must be a project that opens in one of the following environments:  Netbeans, Eclipse, Visual Studio 
or IntelliJ.  You may also have text files together with a Makefile that can be processed by the GNU “make” program 
and compiled using gcc or g++. 

3. You may only use standard libraries (whatever that means).

4. All code must be in a single compressed folder.  There must be one main file (it can have any name) that will be 
judged for the contest.  There may be any number of axillary files.

5. You must include a README file that describes the program, its output and how to compile and run it and that 
identifies the file with the main function/class.

6. The program must be an original program written by you (team entries are allowed and encouraged).  You must have 
all rights to the program.

7. If the editors cannot get your program to compile and run, it will not be considered for the contest.
 
_________
Entries are due by 1 December 2018.  Student entries are encouraged.  
 
Email entries to John Barr at barr@ithaca.edu

Date: Tue Aug 28 04:18:09 2018
To: help2002@csse.uwa.edu.au
Received: from 220.240.47.222
Subject: fgets()
From: chris.mcdonald@uwa.edu.au
X-smilie: none
X-img: none

ANONYMOUS wrote:

> Hi can anyone explain what this function actually does, little confuse here. Does it just 
> read a line up to the null byte character? 

The simplest way to find the answer to your question is to read the online manual entry for 
fgets.  On macOS, the command   man fgets  reports:

    "The fgets() function reads at most one less than the number of char-
     acters specified by size from the given stream and stores them in
     the string str.  Reading stops when a newline character is found, at
     end-of-file or error.  The newline, if any, is retained.  If any
     characters are read and there is no error, a `\0' character is
     appended to end the string."

So, if you're calling  fgets(str, sizeof str, filepointer)

the function will read characters using the filepointer, until it finds a newline sequence or 
the end-of-file condition, copying each read character into your character array, str, and 
appending a NULL-byte character to enable you to treat those characters as a string.


Date: Tue Aug 28 04:20:53 2018
To: help2002@csse.uwa.edu.au
Received: from 220.240.47.222
Subject: No Newline at the end of file
From: chris.mcdonald@uwa.edu.au
X-smilie: none
X-img: none

ANONYMOUS wrote:

> I have tried to compile the file square.c from week 3's Lab, and got an error saying 
> "error: no newline at end of file [-Werror,-Wnewline-eof]", how do I get rid of this 
error?

You get rid of the error by adding a newline at the end of the file (using your text 
editor).  The original file contains a newline character at its end, so how did you 
download/copy it?

Date: Tue Sep  4 13:03:36 2018
To: help2002@csse.uwa.edu.au
Received: from 10.20.167.64
Subject: Look for the explanation for this question
From: poster093@student.uwa.edu.au
X-smilie: coffee.png
X-img: none

This is one of those things you kind of have to know, but you can sort or reason
your way towards the answer.

It is not A as if after an invalid instruction execution was allowed to continue
then this would result in heavily undefined behaviour, as well as possibly being a
security risk.

It would make sense to be B as image you are typing out some document, hit save and
the program crashes and you loose all your work because the drive is full. This
would be very rude and possibly impossible to work around, and OS's try not to be
rude and always try to allow you to recover from possible runtime errors.

I'm not sure what they meant by C exactly, but I think they are not talking about a
sheduleing time limit, more of a stopped responding time limit. Which would make
sense to terminate a program that has stopped responding.

D is a very similar situation to A, and if you spend more time with C/C++ you will
become very familiar with Seg Faults, which are what you get if you try this. 

So from this B is the most reasonable answer

= 120 /10 % 100
= 12 % 100
= 12

Hi Matthew,

Using Edberts easy to read layout, 28 returns the tens digit for both answers.

Assume the variable x is 28:

Using B: 
x % 100 / 10 
= 28 % 100 / 10
= 28 / 10
= 2

Using D:
x / 10 % 100
= 28 /10 % 100
= 2 % 100
= 2

Using B will always give the tens digit.

Using D will always give the hundreds and tens digits for any number over 100.

Fractions are discarded for integer division.

Cheers,

Jason

Date: Thu Sep  6 16:13:11 2018

Yep agree.

You will need something like this for the code to work:

int increment(int i) {
    i++;
    return i;
}
 
void go(void) {
    int i = 0;
    int sum = 0;

    for(i = 0; i < 10; i = increment(i)) {
        sum = sum + i;
    }
    printf("Final value of i = %i\n", i);
}





Date: Thu Sep  6 16:16:29 2018
To: help2002@csse.uwa.edu.au
Received: from 118.209.181.90
Subject: 2017 Test Q12
From: poster092@student.uwa.edu.au
X-smilie: none
X-img: none

Hi, although you are right about void not being able to return a value, it's not 
quite right for this question! So the reason the answer is D (None of the above) is 
because the printed value will always be above 10.

This is because of the sum variable and the way sum and i is expressed.

After the 2nd iteration ( initially i = 0 and sum = 0):

i = 1 and sum = 1

3rd iteration

i = 2 and sum = 3

4th iteration

i = 3 and sum = 6

Here, we can see that the value of sum will be greater than 10 before i reaches 9 
and hence why none of the options are correct except for D.

Date: Thu Sep  6 17:16:18 2018

X-anonymous: yes

Two things to watch for this question.

Firstly, the increment function will successfully increase the value of i in its own 
scope, that is the i value in the function increment() itself, not the i value in 
the for loop in go(). As such, the value of i in the for loop will never change and 
will be stuck in an infinite loop. 

Secondly, even if the i value does increase, the expected output should be 9 as it 
is not printing the sum but the value of "i". 

Date: Thu Sep  6 18:07:37 2018
To: help2002@csse.uwa.edu.au
Received: from 110.175.143.95

X-img: none

>Secondly, even if the i value does increase, the expected output should be 9 as it 
>is not printing the sum but the value of "i". 

You sound educated so I hope you're not trying to mislead people on purpose.

The value of "i" will be 10, not 9 if the "i" value increases by 1 each iteration.

Subject: Windows Subsystem for Linux
From: poster043@student.uwa.edu.au

ANONYMOUS wrote:

> I have found a new windows 10 feature "windows subsystem for linux". Please refer to the link below.
> 
> https://www.howtogeek.com/249966/how-to-install-and-use-the-linux-bash-shell-on-windows-10/
> 
> 
> 
> 
> Do you think this is appropriate to use for the project?
> 
> 
> Thanks in advance.

I have compiled and run the code below using Ubuntu.(although had to go through the manual installation of GCC) It successfully prints out Linux! 

So I think I can use this feature to do the project. 

Good news for windows users!


#include <stdio.h>
#include <stdlib.h>

#include <stdio.h>

/**
 * Determination a platform of an operation system
 * Fully supported supported only GNU GCC/G++, partially on Clang/LLVM
 */

#if defined(_WIN32)
    #define PLATFORM_NAME "windows" // Windows
#elif defined(_WIN64)
    #define PLATFORM_NAME "windows" // Windows
#elif defined(__CYGWIN__) && !defined(_WIN32)
    #define PLATFORM_NAME "windows" // Windows (Cygwin POSIX under Microsoft Window)
#elif defined(__ANDROID__)
    #define PLATFORM_NAME "android" // Android (implies Linux, so it must come first)
#elif defined(__linux__)
    #define PLATFORM_NAME "linux" // Debian, Ubuntu, Gentoo, Fedora, openSUSE, RedHat, Centos and other
#elif defined(__unix__) || defined(__APPLE__) && defined(__MACH__)
    #include <sys/param.h>
    #if defined(BSD)
        #define PLATFORM_NAME "bsd" // FreeBSD, NetBSD, OpenBSD, DragonFly BSD
    #endif
#elif defined(__hpux)
    #define PLATFORM_NAME "hp-ux" // HP-UX
#elif defined(_AIX)
    #define PLATFORM_NAME "aix" // IBM AIX
#elif defined(__APPLE__) && defined(__MACH__) // Apple OSX and iOS (Darwin)
    #include <TargetConditionals.h>
    #if TARGET_IPHONE_SIMULATOR == 1
        #define PLATFORM_NAME "ios" // Apple iOS
    #elif TARGET_OS_IPHONE == 1
        #define PLATFORM_NAME "ios" // Apple iOS
    #elif TARGET_OS_MAC == 1
        #define PLATFORM_NAME "osx" // Apple OSX
    #endif
#elif defined(__sun) && defined(__SVR4)
    #define PLATFORM_NAME "solaris" // Oracle Solaris, Open Indiana
#else
    #define PLATFORM_NAME NULL
#endif

// Return a name of platform, if determined, otherwise - an empty string
char *
get_platform_name() {
    return (PLATFORM_NAME == NULL) ? "" : PLATFORM_NAME;
}

int main(int argc, char *argv[]) {
    puts(get_platform_name());
    return 0;
}

Date: Thu Sep 20 04:44:59 2018
To: help2002@csse.uwa.edu.au
Received: from 220.240.88.17
Subject: Project clarification request
From: chris.mcdonald@uwa.edu.au
X-smilie: none
X-img: none

Hi Matthew,


> Hi, I just have a couple things I wish for clarification on:
> 
> 1) Using make with multiple -f command line flags you are able to select multiple
> makefiles, are we expected to support multiple -f flags in the same way?

Just support a single file provided by the -f option.
If -f is provided multiple times, just process the last file presented:

   ./bake -f file1 -f file2

would just process the spec in file2.

 
> 2)In the project, it says that the -p flag will not actually run the bakefile just
> print internal info and "Then simply exit with success". But with make the -p flag
> still runs the makefile on top of printing internal information. So definitely just
> do what the project says to do?

Yes, just print the internal representation of the spec but do not
execute any target's actions.  In many places, bake's actions are much
simpler (requires less work) than make.

Just to clarify your wording, too;  we don't say that we "run the bakefile" (which 
I've seen confuse some students that believe it needs to be executable).
We run bake, which reads and processes the contents of the bakefile.

Good luck,

void some_function(char **strarray)
{

  strcpy(strarray[M], "string of length 
P-1");
}

char **strarray = malloc(sizeof(char*) * 
N);
some_function(strarray);
//Other code needing strarray not to be

Date: Tue Sep 25 12:31:16 2018
To: help2002@csse.uwa.edu.au
Received: from 130.95.1.70
Subject: Memory allocation inside functions
From: chris.mcdonald@uwa.edu.au
X-smilie: none
X-img: none

Previously, <poster030@student.uwa.edu.au> wrote:

> If memory is allocated to a pointer 
> inside a function is it necessarily not 
> overwritten elsewhere??

Even with your example, it's a bit difficult to understand the question.

If you allocate memory inside a function, and assign that memory's address to a local 
variable or parameter of that function, or to a global variable,  then the allocated 
memory is not overwritten, re-used, or deallocated when the function returns.  Thus you 
can use a function to perform the allocation (and test for its success :-) and then use 
the allocated memory from within another function(s).

You can reason about these semantics because, if it were not the case, then the standard 
function strdup() could not work as expected.

Hope that makes sense.

There is no conflict in the description.  None of the operating system calls that you'll 
require to complete the project are part of the C99 standard library, either, so using

own.

Date: Thu Sep 27 03:25:19 2018

Subject: RAND like variables
From: chris.mcdonald@uwa.edu.au

X-img: none

Hi Matthew,

Answers to both of these questions are specified in the project description:

    "As special cases, the variable expansions $(PID), $(PPID), $(PWD), and $(RAND) are replaced by the 
    results of the function calls getpid(), getppid(), getcwd(), and rand() formatted as strings, 
    respectively."


Previously, <poster093@student.uwa.edu.au> wrote:

> in the project can the special variables like RAND and PID be overridden with user
> define variables?

You could override/redefine the value of, say, PID with your own value
but, as the above states, when $(PID) is expanded it provides the value
given by the function call.

 
> And what has higher priority the special variables or environment variables?

I think you're asking the same question a different way.
The special cases always expand to their function's results.
Consider $(RAND), which should produce a different expansion each time it is requested.

Date: Tue Oct  2 06:29:45 2018
To: help2002@csse.uwa.edu.au
Received: from 203.63.27.152
Subject: Last-Modified header
From: chris.mcdonald@uwa.edu.au
X-smilie: none
X-img: none

Previously, <poster096@student.uwa.edu.au> wrote:

> Is the "Last-Modified" HTTP header the only thing we should consider when looking for when 
> a remote file was last modified?
> 
> And, if so, I'm guessing its an error if that header is not present?

Yes; treat a required but missing header as a form of error.
While curl may report a successful request (giving the response code of 200), not all URLs 
can 'provide' a "Last-Modified" HTTP header.

For example, a URL corresponding to a remote disk file can report a "Last-Modified" value, 
but dynamically generated content does not correspond to an on-disk file,  e.g.

  curl --head http://teaching.csse.uwa.edu.au/units/CITS2002/

reports no "Last-Modified" value because its delivering PHP output.

Date: Tue Oct  2 11:00:13 2018

Subject: Project - variables that aren't defined previously
From: chris.mcdonald@uwa.edu.au

ANONYMOUS wrote:

> What is "the current point"? Is it where the variable is defined or the place where the variable is 
> referenced ($(NAME))?
>  And if it is the latter does this mean to insert an empty string before $(NAME)?

If the line being expanded is:

   aaa$(NAME)bbb

then, if NAME has the value VALUE, the result will be:

   aaaVALUEbbb

If NAME is unknown, the result will be:

   aaabbb

.

Date: Tue Oct  2 11:41:51 2018
To: help2002@csse.uwa.edu.au
Received: from 130.95.1.70
Subject: Project targets
From: chris.mcdonald@uwa.edu.au
X-smilie: none
X-img: none

ANONYMOUS wrote:

> So bake on execution will only build the first target, unless a target is specified on the command line, in 
> which case it will build that target.

Yes, although be careful about your own wording.

In the act of building the required target, bake (or make) may, as a side-effect, build other targets.

Consider examples that we've already seen - in the calcmarks 'project', the required target may be the actual name 
of the final program, but in order to build that target, other targets (such as some object files) may be built in 
determining/evaluating the dependencies of the required target.

Date: Tue Oct  2 19:01:50 2018
To: help2002@csse.uwa.edu.au
Received: from 14.200.213.118
Subject: New line at end of bakefile?
From: chris.mcdonald@uwa.edu.au
X-smilie: none
X-img: none

ANONYMOUS wrote:

> Would it be fair to assume that the 'bakefile' will always have a new (empty) line at the 
> end of the file (e.g. as C files require a blank line at the end)? 

It shouldn't be a problem for your code, even if you encountered one.
If you're reading in each text line with fgets(), each call to fgets() will stop reading when it 
encountered  newline sequence, the end of file, or an error.  So if the last read from your file 
doesn't end in a newline sequence, fgets() will still perform as expected.

C does not require its input files to end with a blank line (a blank line is represented by two 
newline sequences in a row).

Date: Fri Oct  5 05:28:46 2018
To: help2002@csse.uwa.edu.au
Received: from 14.200.213.118
Subject: What does a Bakefile look like?
From: chris.mcdonald@uwa.edu.au
X-smilie: none
X-img: none
X-announcefor: 5d

A number of students have asked what a Bakefile looks like, how it differs from a 
Makefile, and why the project requires you to submit one of each.

The format of a Bakefile (read/understood by bake) is a subset of the possible 
things that can be defined or requested in a Makefile (read/understood by make). 
bake is only supporting a subset of make's features;  similarly a Bakefile can 
only contain a subset of a Makefile's possible contents.  In addition, bake is 
able to recognise that a target's dependency may be a URL; make does not support 
this.

Look at Lecture 12 - the Makefiles (in grey boxes) on p12, p13, and p14 each 
include basic (make) features, and each are also possible Bakefiles.  The 
Makefile on p15 contains an additional feature (automatic variables, on orange) and 
is NOT a possible Bakefile.

Please note that there's a difference in how/when variable expansion is performed 
by bake and make.  With bake, variable expansion is performed at the time the 
$(X) pattern is read/detected - the current value of X, if any, is substituted at 
that time and point. This is true for all Bakefile lines following X's variable 
definition.  In make, variable expansion within actions is delayed until the 
action is executed (this provides make's support for automatic variables, which 
is NOT supported/required in bake).

Date: Sat Oct  6 16:34:49 2018
To: help2002@csse.uwa.edu.au
Received: from 220.240.89.183
Subject: cURL return value
From: chris.mcdonald@uwa.edu.au
X-smilie: none
X-img: none

ANONYMOUS wrote:

> I've thought about using the execl() command and I'm able to successfully run the command 
> from within C and I can see the result of cURL in my terminal window, but I'm not sure how 
> to get the return value into my C program and actually use it.

curl is a utility program (external to bake), so it's RETURN VALUE is really its exit status 
(i.e. did it execute successfully, or not).  That's easy to determine.

However, that's not the question you're asking.
What you're seeing appear on your terminal, printed by curl, is its output, not its return 
value.  Have another look in curl's manual to see how you can capture that, rather than 
having it "disappear" to the screen.

Just use the external program, not libcurl - it's far more difficult to learn and use than 
what you're trying.

When developing your project, before your project is able to build itself, you'll want to use 
make to compile and link the C files (as you've described above).  When your project is 
marked, your Makefile will be used to rebuild your project so that it can be tested.  
Realistically, a number of submitted projects will not work, even to an early stage, and 
submitting a Makefile may help demonstrate some understanding as to how far 'it got'.

Date: Thu Oct 11 21:01:01 2018
To: help2002@csse.uwa.edu.au
Received: from 60.240.239.32
Subject: Reading Makefile/Bakefile
From: chris.mcdonald@uwa.edu.au
X-smilie: none
X-img: none

ANONYMOUS wrote:

> This issue was identified by a tutor and was told to think of another way to implement our 
> code. Currently, our way of identifying the difference between variables, target lines and 
> action lines is checking for '=', ':' and "    "(tab) respectively. However, an issue will 
> arise in this method if the Makefile/Bakefile has a "=" in the action line, or maybe a "    
> "(tab) in the variables line. 

Hello,

Yes, we've been helping a few students in labs this week who are having the same problems or confusion.  It stems 
from trying to take too many shortcuts when processing the input lines.  As you've seen, an action such as

  echo 1+1=2

shouldn't be confused with a variable definition, simply because it has an equals sign in it  (likely detected 
with strchr).

Take a big step backwards and review how to classify every line into one of the possible line types.  Using the 
help described here:

  https://secure.csse.uwa.edu.au/run/help2002?p=np&a=243

we need to first expand any variables in every line, categorise each line, and then process each one based on its 
categorisation.

So we read and process the specification file with:

  while not end of file
    get the next full/continuation line
    expand any variables on that line

    categorise the expanded line:
      if it begins with '#', ignore this line and loop to read the next line

      if the line begins with a tab **and we've just processed a new target**, add this action to that target

      skip any leading spaces or tabs

      if we've reached the end of line, then the whole line was a blank line, so ignore it like a comment line

      now find the first 'word' on the line, collecting its characters until we find a space, tab, =, or :

      if we found '=', call   process_variable_definition(word, rest_of_line),  ideally in a different file

      if we found ':', call   process_target_definition(word, rest_of_line), ideally in a different file

      otherwise the line is unrecognised, print any error message and exit

  fclose file

Hoping that you've also read this:

  https://secure.csse.uwa.edu.au/run/help2002?p=np&a=222

Yes, your Bakefile(s), just like Makefile(s) can specify the build instructions for any project.  We 
usually keep our projects in distinct directories, with one specific Makefile in that directory to 
build that project.

When your bake project is working (even nearly working) you'll be able to copy your project's source 
files, *.c and *.h, to another directory and use/test your bake program on itself:

    mkdir /tmp/testbake
    cp  *.c *.h Bakefile  /tmp/testbake
    ./bake -C /tmp/testbake

And, yes, we'll be testing your project submission on other random files
and projects, too,  but would love to see that it can build itself.

I'm afraid that I can barely understand what you're asking.

One question that I think you're asking, as are some other students, is why or how the people marking your project 
require both a Makefile and a Bakefile.  Yes, they can have identical contents, but they don't have to - for 
example, you may use automatic variables in your Makefile. Moreover, because they have different names, part of 
testing will ensure that your project finds the correct one.

On the issue/confusion that some students have about how the provision/requirement for a Bakefile will help the 
markers, consider the possible test:

    rm -f bake *.o
    cc ...... -o testbake *.c
    rm -f bake *.o
    ./testbake


Date: Sat Oct 13 18:43:00 2018
To: help2002@csse.uwa.edu.au

Subject: Sample output

ANONYMOUS wrote:

> Say we have created our bake.c, its requiring .h file and bakefile and makefile, what 
> should be the output when we input "make" to our terminal?

One of the reasons for setting this type of project, is so that students can get an 
understanding of both the use and working/implementation of a standard utility.  In this case, 
a strong understanding of how 'make' works can provide a lot of understanding about how it 
must (is probably) implemented.  A lot of source code has been freely provided in the unit's 
laboratories and workshops and, so, once you've understood what the project requires, a lot of 
the effort of developing and debugging (and even typing in) the code is simplified.

So you're able, and should, try to answer your own question.
Create your own Makefile which specifies the dependencies you've described in your question, 
and just run "make" to see what output it produces.

Date: Sat Oct 13 18:59:26 2018
To: help2002@csse.uwa.edu.au
Received: from 60.240.239.32
Subject: Storing target dependencies and actions
From: chris.mcdonald@uwa.edu.au
X-smilie: none
X-img: none

ANONYMOUS wrote:

> I think I might have misunderstood/missed that part of the project. So if a : b c, and b has been modified more 
> recently than a, then does b also need to be rebuilt if it has its own line, for example b: d e (regardless of 
> the states of d and e)? I'm not exactly sure how this is meant to work in either Bake or makefiles.


Your original question was (paraphrasing) "do I need to store dependencies and actions, or can I just execute things (if 
necessary) as soon as I read them?"

Consider the Makefile used in Friday's Workshop-8 :

_______
ALL     = list hashtable

C99     = cc -std=c99 -Wall -Werror -pedantic

all:    $(ALL)

list: list.c
        $(C99) -o list list.c

hashtable: hashtable.c
        $(C99) -o hashtable hashtable.c
______

The default target is 'all', and it has two dependencies of (the executable files) 'list' and 'hashtable'.
But neither 'list' or 'hashtable' initially exist (as files), so we can't determine if 'list' or 'hashtable' has been 
modified more recently than 'all', as we don't know what they are. Later in the file (once we read that far) we see that 
'list' is a target which also has dependencies,  and now we can determine whether 'list' needs rebuilding, and we can 
assign/determine list's modification date  (which we required for 'all').

You'll hopefully see that we *do* need to store dependencies and actions as "attributes" of a target, as we don't know 
which we'll need, and in what order, until we've finished reading the whole file, and knowing which targets we need to 
consider.

Try it in the standard utility 'make' to see what is performed, and in what order.
If still struggling with it, try an easy-to-understand tutorial on the standard "make", e.g.

  https://opensourceforu.com/2012/06/gnu-make-in-detail-for-beginners/

> ANONYMOUS wrote:
> 
> > I think I might have misunderstood/missed that part of the project. So if a : b c, and b has been modified more 
> > recently than a, then does b also need to be rebuilt if it has its own line, for example b: d e (regardless of 
> > the states of d and e)? I'm not exactly sure how this is meant to work in either Bake or makefiles.
> 
> 
> Your original question was (paraphrasing) "do I need to store dependencies and actions, or can I just execute things (if 
> necessary) as soon as I read them?"
> 
> Consider the Makefile used in Friday's Workshop-8 :
> 
> _______
> ALL     = list hashtable
> 
> C99     = cc -std=c99 -Wall -Werror -pedantic
> 
> all:    $(ALL)
> 
> list: list.c
>         $(C99) -o list list.c
> 
> hashtable: hashtable.c
>         $(C99) -o hashtable hashtable.c
> ______
> 
> The default target is 'all', and it has two dependencies of (the executable files) 'list' and 'hashtable'.
> But neither 'list' or 'hashtable' initially exist (as files), so we can't determine if 'list' or 'hashtable' has been 
> modified more recently than 'all', as we don't know what they are. Later in the file (once we read that far) we see that 
> 'list' is a target which also has dependencies,  and now we can determine whether 'list' needs rebuilding, and we can 
> assign/determine list's modification date  (which we required for 'all').
> 
> You'll hopefully see that we *do* need to store dependencies and actions as "attributes" of a target, as we don't know 
> which we'll need, and in what order, until we've finished reading the whole file, and knowing which targets we need to 
> consider.
> 
> Try it in the standard utility 'make' to see what is performed, and in what order.
> If still struggling with it, try an easy-to-understand tutorial on the standard "make", e.g.
> 
>   https://opensourceforu.com/2012/06/gnu-make-in-detail-for-beginners/

ANONYMOUS wrote:

> If say '-f' is called multiple times, 
> ie. /bake -f file1 -f -f file2
> 
> Even though we are only to use the final file 'file2', should our program pick up that an 
> argument hasn't been provided to the previous -f call? or should we only check this 
> condition for the last occurence of -f? 

I hadn't considered all of the possible ways in which people could provide erroneous options 
(or how to test for them all).

As the majority of people will be (wisely) using getopt() for their argument parsing, let's 
support what it supports.

In your example, above, getopt() will believe that the name of the required Bakefile is '-f' 
and that the required target is file2.  If both of those exist, then there'd be no error.

I'll provide answers to each of your 3 posts in this one.
It seems like many of your questions have already been asked in this forum, so please read older 
posts.

ANONYMOUS wrote:

> 1.As shown in the last slide of lecture 12 will our bake program have to be able to 
> read and utilise automatic variables.

No; please read  https://secure.csse.uwa.edu.au/run/help2002?hl=look%20like&a=222


> 2.Can we assume that only the action lines will begin with TAB and that no other types 
> of lines will begin with tab

There's no need to assume that, but if it'll help you get past the problems you have with parsing 
the Bakefile then, yes, assume it.
Please read:  https://secure.csse.uwa.edu.au/run/help2002?p=np&a=247&all=y2


> 3.Can i also assume that all the dependencies in the Target lines be separated with a 
> single space

No, and I'm unsure why it should be a big concern. Instead of code like:

    if(*ch == ' ') {
      ++ch;
    }

just write:

    while(*ch == ' ') {
      ++ch;
    }


> I also forgot another question regarding the lines following \
> In make the lines following \ are usually preceded by tabs, for the purpose of our bake 
> program we should also take into accounts these tabs and remove them. Also for space we 
> don't need to remove these just like how make functions correct?

I'm not aware that make (or other utilities) convert those tabs into spaces, but I'll encourage 
you not to worry about these finer points.  They are not going to be tested when marking your 
project.  Simply use the code that's been provided for you, and move on to the more systems-
focused parts of the project:

    https://secure.csse.uwa.edu.au/run/help2002?hl=long%20lines&a=243

> One final question is regarding whether or not Variable Definition lines Target Lines will 
> have any number of tabs and spaces preceding their lines or whether they'll immediately start.

Simply let *any* line commence with zero-or-more spaces or tabs, other than Action lines which 
*must* begin with a tab.  This is described in the pseudo-code, here:

    https://secure.csse.uwa.edu.au/run/help2002?p=np&a=247&all=y2

"When a target is a filename, make compares the timestamps of the target file and its dependency files. If the dependency filename is another target 
in the Makefile, make then checks the timestamps of that target’s dependencies. It thus winds up recursively checking all the way down the 
dependency tree, to the source code files, to see if any of the files in the dependency tree are newer than their target filenames. (Of course, if 
the dependency files don’t exist, then make knows it must start executing the make targets from the “lowest” point in the dependency tree, to create 
them.)"

Date: Fri Oct 19 04:20:43 2018
To: help2002@csse.uwa.edu.au
Received: from 60.240.239.32
Subject: How to modify a char pointer in another function without duplication
From: chris.mcdonald@uwa.edu.au
X-smilie: none
X-img: none

ANONYMOUS wrote:

> Hi,
> 
> I was wondering if it is possible to modify a char pointer in another function by 
> passing its pointer.
> 
> So for example, if I have:
> 
> 
> void addLetter(char *word, char c) {
> Add c to the end of *word
> //also includes reallocation of memory.
> }

Yes, such a function is OK.

 
> void main() {
> Char *word = “cit”;
> addLetter(word, ‘s’);
> print(%s, word)
> 
> //cits is printed out

HOWEVER, the above is not OK.
The string constant "cit" is a CONSTANT - this is, it cannot be modified. The 
compiler will allocate exactly 4 bytes for it (3 + null-byte), so your attempt to 
append an 's' to it will not work as you shouldn't be able to modify/overwrite the 
null-byte.


> However in the function addLetter, it duplicates the word(passed in the 
parameters). 

When you pass a pointer to a function, such as a pointer to a character (a string), 
the string is NOT duplicated in/for the function.  The functions see and, if not 
passed a pointer to read-only memory (such as a constant),  will be able to modify 
the caller's original data.


Date: Fri Oct 19 04:29:52 2018
To: help2002@csse.uwa.edu.au
Received: from 60.240.239.32
Subject: Target as a dependency
From: chris.mcdonald@uwa.edu.au
X-smilie: none
X-img: none

Previously, <poster041@student.uwa.edu.au> wrote:

> The project description specified that dependencies can be 1 of 3 things: a file, an 
> url or another dependency. 

Perhaps a typo, but a dependency CANNOT be another dependency.
I think you meant 'target'.

> In the case that a target is used as a dependency, does that mean that Bake should 
> also build that target (going through its dependency and executing the actions if 
> necessary); or does it mean that we need to know the time that the target was last 
> run and use that to determine whether or not to execute the first target.

If a dependency is another target, you need to check/determine if that target is up-to-date, and 
that may require that target to be rebuilt.


Try the following in make:

targetA: targetB
        @echo action1
        @echo action2

targetB:
        @echo action3
        @echo action4


> targetA was "baked", execute action1 and action2. Does this also mean that we need to 
> store the last time each target was "baked" in the file system?

Each invocation of make or bake is independent;  they do not leave any history/log behind in a 
file.  Each invocation determines what to do, and does it, each time.

Date: Fri Oct 19 04:32:04 2018
To: help2002@csse.uwa.edu.au
Received: from 60.240.239.32
Subject: Circular dependencies in project
From: chris.mcdonald@uwa.edu.au

ANONYMOUS wrote:

> If a Bakefile contains a circular dependency i.e target a is dependent on target b, 
> and target b is dependent on target a, how are we expected to handle it?
> In Make, one of the offending dependencies is removed, but this is a bit tricky, so I 
> was wondering if it would be ok to just print an error message and exit.

No, your project won't be tested with any circular dependencies, so there's no need to 
detect them nor perform any modifications of the dependencies or actions to avoid them.

  https://secure.csse.uwa.edu.au/run/help2002?hl=loop&a=287

ANONYMOUS wrote:

> I was wondering if it was safe to assume that if a dependency has been rebuilt, it has been 
> modified more recently than the target.  In which case, we can use a boolean for whether 
> the target has been rebuilt and not need to use gettimeofday().

Your assumption is a safe one.
However, I'll admit to not having thought through whether a Boolean is sufficient to 
represent the build time (relationship), here - still trying to concoct a scenario where is 
may fail, because a single Boolean value effectively collapses all times to be the same.  If 
using gettimeofday (which is not difficult) at least the passing of time between successive 
calls may take several microseconds.

Date: Fri Oct 19 18:24:33 2018
To: help2002@csse.uwa.edu.au
Received: from 220.253.230.249
Subject: Rebuilding Dependencies and Gettimeofday()
From: poster037@student.uwa.edu.au
X-smilie: none
X-img: none
X-anonymous: yes

Great, thank you very much Chris.  

Yes, I too have spent quite a bit of time racking my brain trying to come up with an example 
where the Boolean will fail.  From my thinking, it seems reasonable to collapse the times, given 
that rebuilding a dependency will always lead to the target needing to be rebuilt - rendering the 
time that the dependency was rebuilt obsolete. 

The only scenario I could see that this would fail is if the target is created during the running 
of bake but before the target is rebuilt, meaning that it is created by the action lines of one 
of its dependency's.  And this seems counter-intuitive to the grouping of the action lines and so 
wouldn't take place in any self-respecting bakefile.  Let me know if you think of another 
scenario that this fails so that I can re-think my method.  

Thanks again.

>

I'm assuming here that you're running  ./bake file1
in both cases.

The physical order of the targets in a Bakefile does not matter and, in your second example, 
target file1 is dependent on the earlier target file2.  As each target can only be defined once in 
each Bakefile, it doesn't matter whether you search forwards or backwards for them.

(but, did you try your own examples with  make ?)

Date: Sun Oct 21 11:59:47 2018
To: help2002@csse.uwa.edu.au
Received: from 60.240.239.32
Subject: Importance of Target Line Order
From: chris.mcdonald@uwa.edu.au
X-smilie: none
X-img: none

ANONYMOUS wrote:

> So, in addition to printing out the error, should we print a list describing the usage of bake (as with Make) and 
> terminate the program, or continue execution with the default bakefile if present?

Certainly report an error, printing out a list of possible options (a 'usage' message) is considered good practice, and 
exit.  DO NOT continue with any default or assumed actions - it's a bad practice for a program to try to outguess the user 
as to 'what they really wanted'.

If excel() fails it's because it couldn't find or run the requested program.

You'll know it has failed because the excel() function will actually return to your code - 
normally, on success, the function does not return.

Rather stumped by this one, as I've never seen fork() fail due to a shortage of 
memory.  The manual entry for fork() states that the only way an ENOMEM error code 
is returned is if there's a shortage of swap space  (not a shortage of RAM).  Are 
you possibly running this on your personal machine, which has a very, very full 
disk?

Date: Tue Oct 23 05:32:38 2018
To: help2002@csse.uwa.edu.au
Received: from 60.240.239.32
Subject: Memory Problem with fork
From: chris.mcdonald@uwa.edu.au
X-smilie: none
X-img: none

Previously, <poster060@student.uwa.edu.au> wrote:

> Yeah previously i was using bufsiz which by 
> default was like 8192 but i set it to 50 and 
> it still won't fork

I don't believe that this (amount of memory) is the reason for your problems with 
fork(), and don't believe that your attempted fix will fix it.

In the grand scheme of things, 8000+ bytes is *tiny* in comparison to the amount 
of memory your process(es) will be using.  If this amount were the problem, you'd 
see that a malloc request of, say, 100000 bytes would fail, but I imagine that 
that still works.

Moreover, you haven't stated how your changed the value from 8192 to 50, but if 
you meant BUFSIZ rather than bufsiz, and if you changed it by editing the stdio.h 
header file, then that's a VERY dangerous practice.  Changing the constant will 
change the size of your own variables that use it, but will not change/reduce the 
size of variables already compiled into the standard libraries.

Date: Tue Oct 23 05:44:29 2018

As the project sheet states, 40 of the possible 60 marks will come from the correctness 
of your solution.  The code for processing the URL  (ignoring the code to fork and exec 
curl, itself, because it's identical that for bash) is fewer than 20 lines long,  and 
will be worth 4 or fewer marks of that 40.

I'm more concerned, however, about your statement "focus more on how to produce object 
files and executable".  Your project does not need to explicitly build any object files 
or executables - bake knows nothing about the compilation process.  Every action line 
(which may have nothing to do with compilation) is simply executed by the shell,  so 
there's nothing you need to specifically do to produce specific files.

Over the past couple of days I've received a number of requests for individual 
extensions for the project.  For about 4 years now, unit coordinators are not permitted

by taking a medical certificate.  Be aware, however, that being busy with other 
assessments in other units, or being busy in paid employment is not considered as 
grounds for an extension, and you may consider the 10%-per-day late penalty a better 
option if needing a bit more time.

Previously, <poster000@student.uwa.edu.au> wrote:

> Not sure how to execvp the exit command. Get the error of file path not found which 
> is expected since exit is in stlib.h i believe.

Not sure I quite understand the question.
However, you won't need to use execvp(),  but should be using execl().
There is no program named 'exit';  the standard shells are able execute 'exit' because they 
call the exit() function, internally, to exit the shell itself.

(I also don't understand the title of this thread).


> PS: how to change from clang to gcc in apple comp? Can't seem to even run the 
> calcmarks makefile without it erroring.
> 
> All my code written for LINUX Ubuntu and seems stable so far but can't seem to test 
> on Mac (I don't own any).

For each of macOS or Linux, do not stipulate which compiler you want - just invoke 'cc'.

Date: Wed Oct 24 04:06:32 2018
To: help2002@csse.uwa.edu.au
Received: from 60.240.239.32
Subject: Error reporting and length of lines
From: chris.mcdonald@uwa.edu.au
X-smilie: none
X-img: none

ANONYMOUS wrote:

> Is there a preference between using perror or manually using fprintf to print a custom 
> message to stderr?

perror() works correctly when reporting errors detected with calls to standard library 
functions or system calls.  It cannot be used to print your own custom messages.

> Also a question about coding style, will we get penalised if any of our lines of code are 
> over 80 characters or long or is it fine as long as overall the code is readable?

No penalties for longer lines, but readability is essential
(no penalties for anything - you earn marks, you don't have them taken away).


Date: Wed Oct 24 04:11:32 2018

Previously, <poster051@student.uwa.edu.au> wrote:

> Is it ok if we use functions that require feature test macros? I'm specifically trying to use 
> fdopen but I need to add the line "#include _XOPEN_SOURCE 600" before I include stdio.h 
> for it to work. Should we avoid these functions or is this normal/acceptable?

I understand why you're asking the question but, as for strdup() on Linux, I find it much 
easier to just declare it:

   extern FILE *fdopen(int fd, char *mode);


(there's also differing claims to its ownership - XOPEN or POSIX).

 From your description of the problem, alone, I can't diagnose the problem, but it's extremely unlikely that you've stumbled on a bug in mktime(), given the 
billions of times it's used by others, worldwide, everyday.  The problem is almost certainly elsewhere in your program.

At this late stage it's not going to give much away, but I've written a bit of code:

    else if(strncasecmp(line, "last-modified:", 14) == 0) {
	struct tm tm;

	printf("%s\n", line);
	if(strptime(line+20, TIME_FORMAT_STRING, &tm) != NULL) {
	    result      = mktime(&tm);
	    printf("\ttime_t %i\n", (int)result);
	    printf("\tlocal %s", ctime(&result));
	}
    }

and call it hundreds of times in a standalone program;
it keeps printing, without failing on either macOS and Linux:

Should we expect to be tested "Makefile/Bakefile" that is different from the 
Makefiles in Lecture12 p.12,13,14?
What I meant by different is "a comment line within the same line as variable, 
target or command lines" instead of above or below this lines or on a separate line.
This is the only variation that I can think of.
or
Should we be fine if we can do the followings?
1. variable substitutions
2. memory allocations
3. curling urls
4. modification time comparisons

e.g 
# A Makefile to build our 'calcmarks' project

PROJECT =  calcmarks #name of the project, run this by: ./calcmarks RESULT.txt
HEADERS =  $(PROJECT).h #this is a header
OBJ     =  calcmarks.o globals.o readmarks.o correlation.o #object files


C99     =  cc -std=c99 #this is a comment
CFLAGS  =  -Wall -pedantic -Werror #this is a comment


$(PROJECT) : $(OBJ)#this is a comment
       $(C99) $(CFLAGS) -o $(PROJECT) $(OBJ) -lm #this is a comment


calcmarks.o : calcmarks.c $(HEADERS) #this is a comment
       $(C99) $(CFLAGS) -c calcmarks.c #this is a comment

globals.o : globals.c $(HEADERS) #this is a comment
       $(C99) $(CFLAGS) -c globals.c #this is a comment

readmarks.o : readmarks.c $(HEADERS) #this is a comment
       $(C99) $(CFLAGS) -c readmarks.c #this is a comment

correlation.o : correlation.c $(HEADERS)#this is a comment
       $(C99) $(CFLAGS) -c correlation.c #this is a comment


clean:
       rm -f $(PROJECT) $(OBJ)

ANONYMOUS wrote:

> I just have a couple of quick questions about the project arguments.
> First is how should our program react when the input is both -s and -n as they're
> conflicting arguments, as one of them is print executing commands but don't execute
> them, whilst the other is execute commands and don't print.

The -n option still performs everything 'normally', it simply does not execute the action lines.
The -s option suppresses the printing of the actions before they are executed.
With just -n, the actions are still printed.
With both -n and -s, the actionsa re not printed.

> --------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------
-----
> Second is the interaction between -n and @ action lines, in this case I'm assuming
> that -n should take priority and the @ action lines are still printed.

Same mechanism as above;  @ simply requests 'silence' from a single action.

> --------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------
-----
> Third is will we need to code for "multi level" dependencies. For example
> PrimaryTarget : D1 D2 D3
> D3 : D5 D6
> D2: D3 D4 D5
> D5 : D6

Yes, absolutely.

> --------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------
----
> Fourth, I'm just wondering if it's fine that when I'm running -s if it's fine for my
> program when using execl to grab the header files for url it's okay to print out the
> output of the command to the terminal as I'm not sure how to stop it from printing
> in the terminal.

Previously answered.
Re-read the manual entry for curl to learn how to capture its output into a file.

> --------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------
----
> Fifth I have an error check for testing whether or not we fail to pull the header
> information from the url, and was wondering how it would interact with -i. As if we
> fail to get the headerfile we won't be able to determine whether or not the
> actionLine needs to be run in the first place. So should the program just terminate.
> In general should -i only allow errors for action Lines but not allow errors for
> anything else.
> --------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------
----

The -i option only ignores the exit status of actions, so this answer applies:

  https://secure.csse.uwa.edu.au/run/help2002?p=np&a=361&all=y2

ANONYMOUS wrote:

> What is the best way to count the number of characters, for example count the number of 
> colons in a file??

Read the file line by line.

Use an int variable called count to count the number of colons.

For each line, iterate through each char (preferably using a char pointer) and every time you find 
a ':' increase count by one.

Then just return the value of count when all lines in your file have been read.


Date: Wed Oct 24 17:51:04 2018
To: help2002@csse.uwa.edu.au

Subject: How to get rid of MULTIPLE SPACES on c????
From: poster074@student.uwa.edu.au
X-smilie: none
X-img: none
X-anonymous: yes

ANONYMOUS wrote:

> 	$(C99)
> ^^ I want to clear the spaces here just so I have the $(C99) variable.
> 
> Thanks!

I am assuming you want something just to "get rid" of the white spaces, before the first 
word in a line.

You can use something like this:

   while(isspace(*ch) == " "){
       ch++;
   }

As soon as the while loop breaks you are at the first char in the line and then you can do 
whatever you intend to.

>        ch++;

I suggest that you change this to:

     while(isspace(*ch)) {
         ch++
     }

as isspace() is only defined to return 0 or non-zero, not the character it matches.

 From the above link you will see...the commented conversation

/*************************************************
Hi Matthew,


> Hi, I just have a couple things I wish for clarification on:
> 
> 1) Using make with multiple -f command line flags you are able to select multiple
> makefiles, are we expected to support multiple -f flags in the same way?

Just support a single file provided by the -f option.
If -f is provided multiple times, just process the last file presented:

   ./bake -f file1 -f file2

would just process the spec in file2.

/**********
command/action line for "clean" target in the Bakefile/bakefile ***must*** be: 
clean: 
      rm -f *.o 
(or)
clean:
      rm -f $(OBJECTS) //OBJECTS = a.o b.o c.o d.o e.o
(or) 
clean:
      rm -f a.o b.o c.o d.o e.o
**********/

Assuming working in the terminal:

Line1: prompt>make
Line2: make: 'bake' is up to date.
Line3: prompt>./bake clean 
Line4: rm -f a.o b.o c.o d.o e.o

Otherwise, it can't rebuild itself in the same directory if the following command line is passed 
within the same directory. 

Line1: prompt>./bake clean
Line2: rm -f bake a.o b.o c.o d.o e.o

Date: Thu Oct 25 10:25:19 2018
To: help2002@csse.uwa.edu.au
Received: from 130.95.1.70
Subject: URL Dependencies
From: chris.mcdonald@uwa.edu.au
X-smilie: none
X-img: none

ANONYMOUS wrote:

> Hi, 
> 
> I am a little confused on how exactly to handle URL dependencies. Are we supposed to 
> rebuild *all* targets that have a URL dependency that is newer than it (even though the URL 
> doesn't appear in the action line), or should we just print a message stating that the URL 
> is newer? 

There's nothing special about URLs as dependencies.
Dependencies are each one of: a file, a target, or a URL,
and each has a special/different way to find its modification time.

Independent of the what the dependencies are, we only build the minimum number of targets to 
complete what bake is asked to do (either a specific target or the default/first one).

If a URL can't be found or doesn't provide a Last-Modified line, then it's the same error condition 
as if a required target couldn't be found.

Date: Thu Oct 25 12:59:40 2018
To: help2002@csse.uwa.edu.au
Received: from 130.95.1.70
Subject: further clarification
From: chris.mcdonald@uwa.edu.au
X-smilie: none
X-img: none

ANONYMOUS wrote:

> prompt>./bake //searches only Bakefile/bakefile by default in the same director

Yes, from the current directory in which bake was invoked.

 
> prompt>./bake -C [directory] //searches only Bakefile/bakefile by default in the specified [directory]

Change the process's current working directory, and continue as with your first example.
The directory name is not optional.

 
> prompt>./bake -f [target] //target can be Bakefile/bakefile/Makefile/makefile/"any file" in the same directory

NO.
You need to invoke:     ./bake -f filename  [target]
The optional target is a target-name that must be found in the indicated file.


> prompt>./bake -C [directory] -f [target] //target can be Bakefile/bakefile/Makefile/makefile/"any file" in the 
> specified [directory]

The command  ./bake -C directoryname -f filename

first changes to the requested directory, and then reads from filename in the new directory.

Should we expect both the cases especially for ***urls***?

******************Case 1*************************
# A Makefile to build our 'calcmarks' project
PROJECT =  calcmarks
HEADERS =  $(PROJECT).h
OBJ     =  calcmarks.o globals.o readmarks.o correlation.o
URL1    =  http://teaching.csse.uwa.edu.au/units/CITS2002/project/dependency-5m
URL2    =  http://teaching.csse.uwa.edu.au/units/CITS2002/project/dependency-20m
URL3    =  http://teaching.csse.uwa.edu.au/units/CITS2002/project/dependency-4h
C99     =  cc -std=c99
CFLAGS  =  -Wall -pedantic -Werror

$(PROJECT) : $(OBJ)  $(URL1) $(URL2)
       $(C99) $(CFLAGS) -o $(PROJECT) $(OBJ) -lm
...

correlation.o : correlation.c $(HEADERS) $(URL3)
       $(C99) $(CFLAGS) -c correlation.c 
...
****************end of Case 1********************
******************Case 2*************************
PROJECT =  calcmarks
HEADERS =  $(PROJECT).h
OBJ     =  calcmarks.o globals.o readmarks.o correlation.o
C99     =  cc -std=c99
CFLAGS  =  -Wall -pedantic -Werror

$(PROJECT) : $(OBJ)
       $(C99) $(CFLAGS) -o $(PROJECT) $(OBJ) -lm
...
globals.o : globals.c $(HEADERS)   http://teaching.csse.uwa.edu.au/units/CITS2002/project/dependency-5m
       $(C99) $(CFLAGS) -c globals.c

readmarks.o : readmarks.c $(HEADERS)   http://teaching.csse.uwa.edu.au/units/CITS2002/project/dependency-20m
       $(C99) $(CFLAGS) -c readmarks.c

correlation.o : correlation.c $(HEADERS) http://teaching.csse.uwa.edu.au/units/CITS2002/project/dependency-4h
       $(C99) $(CFLAGS) -c correlation.c
...
**************end of Case 2**************************

Do I miss out anything about getopt()? Getopt in our project should support Line 1, 2, 3 as shown below but not

Line1: prompt>./bake -f file1 -f file2 //only file2 is used->SUCCESS

Line2: prompt>./bake -f file1 -f file2 -f file3 //only file3 is used->SUCCESS

Line3: prompt>./bake -f file1 target1 //SUCCESS

Line4: prompt>./bake -f file1 target1 target2 //FAILURE

Line5: prompt>./bake -f file1 target1 -f file2 target2 //FAILURE

Date: Thu Oct 25 15:40:54 2018
To: help2002@csse.uwa.edu.au
Received: from 130.95.1.70
Subject: further clarification
From: chris.mcdonald@uwa.edu.au
X-smilie: none
X-img: none

ANONYMOUS wrote:

> Do I miss out anything about getopt()? Getopt in our project should support Line 1, 2, 3 as shown below but not 
> Line 4 & 5.

getopt() doesn't perform any magic, it only helps you out in parsing the arguments.
It will report failure if the provided arguments do not match the pattern passed as a parameter to getopt().
For example,  if you run bake as   ./bake -f
but without a filename following, getopt() will detect this, and inform your code about the problem,
but it doesn't report/print any message about the missing filename.

Similarly, our project should only accept zero-or-more targets on the command-line so, if two are given, then your code 
- not getopt() - needs to detect that and report the problem.

Yes, needs to be fixed.
Very likely a memory allocation error.

 
> Also, when using calloc for an array of strings (pointer to a pointer **), how much space 
> do we allocate to the outer calloc() and the inner calloc()?

Perhaps a misunderstanding of this is leading to your error?
You need to allocate  (N * sizeof(char *)) bytes for your vector of pointers,
and then use strdup() to allocate space for, and clone/duplicate into, each string you're vector 
will be pointing to.

Date: Fri Oct 26 04:54:26 2018
To: help2002@csse.uwa.edu.au

Subject: Question about targets
From: chris.mcdonald@uwa.edu.au
X-smilie: none
X-img: none

ANONYMOUS wrote:

> If one of the dependencies of target A is another target, then are the action lines of 
> target A executed regardless of A's other dependencies?

Not necessarily;  if the 'other target' does not need rebuilding, then (with respect to 
*that* dependency) A does not need rebuilding and, thus, its actions are not executed.


Date: Fri Oct 26 05:05:16 2018
To: help2002@csse.uwa.edu.au
Received: from 60.240.239.32
Subject: Can we use libraries from GitHub?
From: chris.mcdonald@uwa.edu.au
X-smilie: none
X-img: none

ANONYMOUS wrote:

> We have decided to store our dependancies in a hashtable and are using uthash 
> from GitHub to achieve this. Is this allowed?

At this late stage, assuming that you've already adopted the Github code and it's 
"locked in" to your project, I feel I have to say yes.


______
BUT, I seriously question why you'd come to this decision:

- code to store the names and values in a simple 'vector of structures' approach, 
as promoted in our lectures and workshops (our concordance exercise), has all but 
been provided. 

- a typical Makefile or Bakefile will have fewer than 10 variable definitions, so 
storing the names and values in a hashtable, rather than a simple vector, will 
use hundreds (thousands?) of times more memory for just a handful of elements.

- and if you wanted a hashtable, with code simple enough to understand, a good 
example was provided in our last workshop.

Using getopt(), as strongly encouraged, provides great support for concatenated 
options.  Use getopt() and the difficulty in your question disappears.

However, unsure if this demonstrates some confusion, but your example

  bake -C -f -s directoryname filename

will not work as you may expect, as the '-f' will be assumed to be the name of 
the directory for the '-C' option.



Date: Fri Oct 26 05:16:00 2018

>

If a URL is not found, just as if a target is not found, print an error and exit (as does make).

The project description states that if a URL does not exist, then you should not try to find that same 
URL as a target.  URLs are only ever dependencies, and are never targets.

Date: Fri Oct 26 08:42:39 2018

From: chris.mcdonald@uwa.edu.au
X-smilie: none
