ANONYMOUS wrote:

> 1. forking the action, and executing the action in the child process.

I hope that you mean "forking, running bash in the child process, and letting bash 
execute the action".


> 2. Then we use WEXITSTATUS and WIFEXITED to try and catch any of the errors.

Capturing the exit status of bash (which will be the exit status of the action that 
bash tried to execute)?

 
> 3. Either exiting with success or failure, but our problem is that it still prints 
> the errors for such as compiling problems in a dependency and if my understanding is 
> correct, this should not be printed.

The -i option does not require you to suppress any error output (as that is very 
helpful).  The -i option just asks bake to treat any 'failed' actions as if they 
succeeded.

**********************************************
10.2.4 The following consequences of late or non-submission must be made clear to 
students:

 

a) a penalty of 10 per cent of the possible mark allocated for the assessment item is 
deducted per day for the first 7 days (including weekends and public holidays), after 
which the assigned work is not accepted. Each 24-hour block is recorded from the time the 
assignment is due. For example, a student, who has submitted a piece of work for an 
assessment item late by three days and was scored a mark of 45 out of a possible mark of 
50, receives after penalty a mark of 30 out of 50 (a mark of 5 is deducted per day as a 
penalty for each student from the mark obtained). Where there are a number of tasks within 
an assessment item, the late penalty may be applied holistically to the assessment item 
after all tasks have been completed.

b) where assignments are graded Pass/Fail, failure to submit the assignment in time may 
result in the student not being permitted to take the final exam at the end of the 
semester or a teaching period and being unable to progress to the next level of the course 
or to graduate in the case where it is the final unit of the course; and

c) assessments submitted later than 7 days after the deadline receive a mark of zero, 
unless an application for mitigation is approved in accordance with the University Policy 
on Assessment: Special Consideration (UP11/23).
*********************************************

Hello Angus,

That's an interesting observation but, I'm afraid that I don't see the connection.

I'm not going to claim this is the best way to do it, or even a *good* way to do it, but I used a 
combination of a new static char that's far larger than it needs to be, strncpy, and sprintf. Find 
the index of the variable using strstr, copy the first (var_index-old_line) characters from old 
line to the new line using strncpy, null terminate the new string, then construct it all using 
sprintf. 

I think that's how it works, anyway. It's far from perfect, but hopefully that keeps you going with 
the project!

Date: Sat Oct 27 19:05:05 2018
To: help2002@csse.uwa.edu.au
Received: from 60.240.239.32
Subject: Late submission 
From: chris.mcdonald@uwa.edu.au
X-smilie: none
X-img: none

ANONYMOUS wrote:

> basically I can't figure out how to change only part of a string ie. from index 8 onwards, 
> or how to capture only part of a string. i know there are functions like strtok or strstr 
> but surely there is a simpler way that i'm just blind to seeing.

You've likely submitted your project by now, but don't forget that strings are (can also be accessed 
as) arrays of characters, so you can to just part of a string, between "$(" and ")", character-by-
character by accessing each array index.  Copy the required characters into another array, terminate 
that with a null-byte, and you can then use that as a string to find it in your list of variables.

Don't forget that a variable's name might be much shorter than its value, so you won't be able to just 
replace it in-situ.

Good luck,

Date: Sat Oct 27 19:07:27 2018
To: help2002@csse.uwa.edu.au
Received: from 60.240.239.32
Subject: Targets with no dependencies
From: chris.mcdonald@uwa.edu.au
X-smilie: none
X-img: none

ANONYMOUS wrote:

> On the project page, it seems to indicate that lines declaring targets with no 
> dependencies should always trigger the execution of the succeeding action lines. 
> Is that what we're meant to do, seeing that make itself ignores such lines?

make doesn't ignore targets with no dependencies.
Consider the target named 'clean' here:

  http://teaching.csse.uwa.edu.au/units/CITS2002/lectures/lecture12/14.html

Executing 'make clean' will *always* run the target's action, because there's no file named 
'clean' in the current directory   (make calls these phony targets).

ANONYMOUS wrote:

> Can we safely assume that, if the target we're handling isn't an existing file, we 
> should always execute that target's actions? And either way, would you recommend

> exist on file?

Executing a target's actions depends on that target's dependencies (if any), not whether or not 
it's an existing file.  See:

  https://secure.csse.uwa.edu.au/run/help2002?p=np&a=442

A target's actions may not always create a file with the target's name, so you may find it 
easier to think of things as a 'rebuild time' rather than a modification times.

Date: Sat Nov  3 11:17:59 2018
To: help2002@csse.uwa.edu.au
Received: from 10.20.193.34
Subject: small query
From: poster027@student.uwa.edu.au
X-smilie: none
X-img: none
X-anonymous: yes

in lecture 21 slide 02 referencing bitwise operators in C99:

Name : example: result:        description:
"not":      ~3:	    -4:	  Inverts the bits:

tilde3, as I understand, inverts the bits ie. 011 becomes 100 (3 becomes 4) 

can anyone explain why the result is negative? thanks

Date: Sat Nov  3 15:11:24 2018
To: help2002@csse.uwa.edu.au
Received: from 106.68.110.23
Subject: small query
From: poster093@student.uwa.edu.au
X-smilie: coffee.png
X-img: none

If we were dealing with unsigned integers, integers which cannot be negative, then
you would be correct in the case of 3 bit sized unsigned ints. So for a 1 byte
unsigned it:

~1 => 254  which is:  ~00000001 => 11111110

However, in this case, we are talking about singed int's, so they can be negative.
The way this information is almost always stored is in the most significant bit (the
left most in this layout). So since that one will be flipped too, it becomes a
negative, and then it is not as simple as 0xxxxxxx = - 1xxxxxxx, e.g 10000001 != -1,
rather it equals -127. This is because it basically starts count up from the
smallest negative.

So in signed:

~1 => -2   which is the same as before, bitwise: ~00000001 => 11111110

Date: Sat Nov  3 19:10:20 2018
To: help2002@csse.uwa.edu.au

for this question:

Date: Thu Nov  8 17:40:49 2018
To: help2002@csse.uwa.edu.au
Received: from 130.95.1.70
Subject: Question in 2017 exam
From: chris.mcdonald@uwa.edu.au
X-smilie: none
X-img: none

ANONYMOUS wrote:

> are we expected to have 2 advantages and 2 disadvantages for both paging 
> and segmentation for a total of 8? Or is one each enough?

No (not 8); I don't think such questions are very good at discriminating a student's 
knowledge - a point that is listed as an advantage for one mechanism can often simply be 
listed as a disadvantage for the other mechanism.  Also, requiring a nice even number of 
mirrored adv/disadv suggests that each mechanisms is equal in value.

Both mechanisms hold some (common) advantages over a system that used neither mechanism, 
and each mechanism has advantage(s) over the other mechanism. You may not get to 8, but 
there's enough to write about.  But focus on the adv/disadv,  not waste time and space 
describing how each mechanism works.

From: chris.mcdonald@uwa.edu.au

Hello Everyone,

Just an update on the progress of the project marking (a popular question being asked by 
email).

The marking of the projects by manual inspection (looking at programming style and good 
practices for this project, providing 20/60 marks has been completed).  However, attempts 
to automate most of the tests, to see which parts of your projects work successfully 
(providing 40/60 of the marks), have proved too difficult (or error prone), and they're 
mostly being checked "by hand".  So, you won't be getting your project marks back today,  
but will some time over the weekend.

[ Why has the automated testing proved more difficult than hoped, and what sort of things 
really slow down the marking?

We've received 168 project submissions, 68 from individuals, 100 from pairs.
Of those, 22 clearly look like they have never compiled+linked successfully, and no 
(reasonable) amount of editing can get them to compile;  18 submissions were missing 
critical files (typically 1 or 2), and students were emailed to provide the files; 28 
submissions required some (limited) editing/modification to just get them to compile; 25 
submissions did not include the required Makefile (or even a Makefile.txt) to compile them; 
15 submissions incorrectly required their Bakefiles to have a .txt extension.  All of these 
problems need addressing even before a project can be run against even basic tests.

And, sadly, 21 students' submissions have been referred to the Faculty's Academic Conduct 
advisor for excessive collaboration, plagiarism, or contract cheating. ]

Date: Sun Nov 11 12:09:18 2018
To: help2002@csse.uwa.edu.au
Received: from 60.241.152.150
Subject: How in-depth should the OS answers be?
From: chris.mcdonald@uwa.edu.au
X-smilie: none
X-img: none

ANONYMOUS wrote:

> If my answer to one of these questions contains a lot of technical jargon, should I then 
> explain what each technical word means or assume that you already know what it means? I.E 
> Should I write my answer on the assumption that I'm answering to someone who has a 
> background in these areas already or on the assumption that the reader has no background?  

With your answer you're explaining to a knowledgable marker what you know.
You will be awarded marks for how well (correctly, clearly, unambiguously) you address the question. 

You are not being asked to provide a basic tutorial-style introduction to a person unfamiliar to an 
area;  you are not being awarded marks for how ell you educate that person in the material for the 
first time.

However, I think I can see why you're asking your question, the "a lot of jargon" part (let's call 
them 'terms' :-)

You should use the correct technical terms to demonstrate your understanding of a topic, but it's 
important to place them in a context, so that you're not just 'name dropping' to make your answer 
appear knowledgable.

(I see the above point, a lot, in the Computer Networks unit which employs lots of acronyms to 
succinctly describe technical concepts;  too often in exams students use the acronyms as a form of 
'name dropping', often evident when the wrong acronym is used).

Does that help?

Date: Sun Nov 11 12:11:56 2018
To: help2002@csse.uwa.edu.au
Received: from 60.241.152.150
Subject: Including import statements in code
From: chris.mcdonald@uwa.edu.au
X-smilie: none
X-img: none

The above response is the correct one :-)
though remember that, while Java and Python, have an 'import' construct, C has aa 
'#include' construct with quite a different role and use.

In the 2017 exam student solutions he showed us in week 12, the 2 examples that the student 
provide were(kinda) 2 of the dot point (1 and 3) mentioned in Lecture 14 page 1 slide.

--> Except for infrequent branches and function/procedure invocation, program execution is 
sequential. The next instruction to be fetched usually follows the last one executed.

--> Programs generally operate at the same "depth" of function-invocation. References to 
instructions cluster within (and between) a small collection of functions.

--> Most iterative control flow (looping) is over short instruction sequences. Instructions 
from the same memory locations are fetched several times in succession.

--> Access to memory locations holding data is, too, constrained to a few frequently required 
data structures, or sequential steps through memory (e.g. when traversing arrays)

And the student got 4/5 marks for this questions, so I assume he's on the right track?

Previously, <poster020@student.uwa.edu.au> wrote:

> Thank you very much to Chris and colleagues for taking up the immense challenge of 
> "manually marking" our projects in order to assign additional marks.

Thanks Jason.

The marking of the project is being reviewed (and not as quickly as I understand students would prefer), more 
out of necessity than generosity.

The part being reviewed is the testing of the compiled programs (/40). The first 4 marks are awarded based on 
whether the submissions would compile and link cleanly (no warnings), 4/4,  or if they required a small amount 
of editing to get them to compile.

But based on the other 20 tests, each time executing the compiled program in a new empty directory, the 
average mark, so far, is only a bit over 11 / 36.
___
x / 4      Compiles and links successfully, without warnings, no changes required
x / 2      Test 1 - reports an error if either Bakefile or bakefile does not exist
x / 2      Test 2 - reports an error if Bakefile contains just plain text
x / 2      Test 3 - a basic Bakefile, 1 target, 0 dependencies, 1 successful action
x / 2      Test 4 - target does not exist, 0 dependencies, 2 successful actions
x / 2      Test 5 - target does not exist, 0 dependencies, actions are: success,fail,success
x / 2      Test 6 - target does not exist, existing dependency, 1 action
x / 2      Test 7 - target does not exist, 2 existing dependency, 1 action
x / 2      Test 8 - new target, 1 older dependency, 1 action
x / 2      Test 9 - request a target that does not appear in Bakefile
x / 2      Test 10 - request the second target that exists in Bakefile
x / 2      Test 11 - request default target that builds a second target
x / 1      Test 12 - defines 1 variable, but does not later expand it
x / 1      Test 13 - defines 1 variable, expands it later
x / 1      Test 14 - defines 2 variables, expands both of them
x / 1      Test 15 - expands the 4 builtin variables: PID, PPID, PWD, and RAND
x / 2      Test 16 - the -C command-line option (change directory before reading Bakefile)
x / 2      Test 17 - the -f command-line option (read specifications from indicated filename)
x / 2      Test 18 - the -p command-line option (print internal representation of specifications)
x / 2      Test 19 - a dependency is a URL, which forces rebuilding target
x / 2      Test 20 - a dependency is a URL, which does not force rebuilding target
___

So I've been looking for any systemic errors or misunderstandings that have 'prevented' many submissions from 
receiving any marks from about Test 4.  So far, the only systemic problem appears to be that many submissions 
have made no use of the sample code and sample solutions provided in lectures, labsheets, workshops, and 
help2002,  and the reinvention-of-the-wheel has introduced many errors that, clearly, students have spent much 
time trying to correct (and, thus, not progressing through the project's requirements).

On that, I'm quite stumped.

Date: Tue Nov 20 15:28:18 2018
To: help2002@csse.uwa.edu.au
Received: from 130.95.1.70
Subject: Project marks available
From: chris.mcdonald@uwa.edu.au
X-smilie: none
X-img: none
X-announcefor: 6d

Your project marks and feedback sheets are now available via csmarks.
The average of all 143 submitted, compilable, and testable projects is a 
disappointingly low 28.1 / 60.

Firstly, congratulations to the 33 students receiving 45 or higher for the project.  
Obviously a job well done, and they were a pleasure to mark.

All submissions have received some marks (average of 12.2 /20) for programming 
style, including your use of meaningful comments, well chosen identifier names, 
appropriate choice of basic data-structures and data-types, appropriate choice of 
control-flow constructs, and isolation of distinct responsibilities in separate 
source files.

Up to 4 marks have been awarded for submitting a project which simply compiled and 
linked correctly (free marks for a 2nd year unit!).  If I had to make a small 
correction to your submission to get it to compile and link, or if I had to 
construct a Makefile for you (as one was required), then you lost a mark for each 
instance.

You may see, download, and run a (cleaned up version of) the marking script here:

  http://teaching.csse.uwa.edu.au/units/CITS2002/project/markme.sh

(careful to preserve tabs in the file when you download it).

If your project could be compiled and tested, it was executed against each of 20 
tests. Each test was performed in a fresh directory, with a single Bakefile created 
just for that test. Your project received either 2,1, or 0 marks for each test, 
depending on whether it produced/printed the expected result/answer, appeared to do 
nothing, or whether it crashed.  All debug output produced by your project (which 
killed the opportunity for much automated marking) was ignored.

If your submission received 0/40 for compiling (0/4) and testing (0/36), then it is 
genuinely because your submission produced so many compilation errors that it looks 
like it has never been compiled, and I couldn't understand your logic and correct 
the errors, in a reasonable time, to successfully compile it to undertake some 
testing.  But I honestly don't think that is my role when marking, and certainly not
for a 2nd year unit.  If you believe you've been harshly done by in receiving 0/40
for this 2nd part, please contact me with your reasoning, else your mark will stand.

There are a number of submissions that have compiled and can successfully run the 
first test or two, but then fail most subsequent tests in almost the same way each 
time - a segmentation violation or an abort trap (usually from accessing an invalid 
pointer), or with a malloc/realloc/free error.  I have resolved about 20 of these 
cases already - to enable them to run and pass more tests - but for others it's 
difficult to award them any marks for testing. After exam marking is finished, I'll 
have another look at some (of the challenging) ones.  If, since submission, you've 
discovered a short/simple fix to your project, please let me know.

If your mark is listed as NOT SUBMITTED it's because none of the submissions 
included your student number (or a number close to it). If you submitted a project 
individually, then you may have submitted it to the wrong place (another unit?).  If 
you worked with someone else then it's most likely they forgot to include your 
details on the submission.  In either case, let me know the story and it can be 
resolved.

If your mark is listed as WITHHELD then, I'm afraid, I've referred you (and likely 
your project partner) to the Faculty's Academic Conduct Advisor for submitting work 
too similar to that of another submission, or in cases where I've been able to 
identify contract cheating.  The Faculty Office will be in contact with you shortly, 
if they haven't done so already.  Please do not contact me about your case, as I 
must remain independent of the decision process.

---
If you believe that there's been an error in your project marking, please run your
submitted project against the provided marking script, and we can resolve any issues
(preferably via email, not through the help forum).

But *before* you email to report a concern about your mark, please run your
submitted code against the provided script (above) and view its output.
Instructions appear at the top of the script.


Good luck,

Date: Tue Nov 20 18:19:30 2018
To: help2002@csse.uwa.edu.au
Received: from 130.95.1.70
Subject: Unit Marking 
From: chris.mcdonald@uwa.edu.au
X-smilie: none
X-img: none

ANONYMOUS wrote:

>  From what I known is that the average of a level 2 unit must lie in between 63-68? 
> 
> I would appreciate if someone could tell what would most likely happen in 
> circumstances where the average of unit does not fall into this bracket? Thanks 

Units are only scaled if they need to be (which makes sense).

For our Faculty, the required range for a Level-2 unit is 65.0%→70.0%
and, if results are outside of the range, they can either be moderated to sit exactly 
at the bottom or the top of the range (not changed to 'magically' sit somewhere in 
the middle), or an argument can be made as to why the marks are 'correct' and should 
not be moderated.

Other than for any individual errors that may be identified, yes, the
marks for the unit are finalised  (they have to be finalised before
being released).

The unit average for all students, including those receiving zeros for
not submitting the project (37) or being absent from the final exam (26),
was 62.0%.

The average for all students meeting the Faculty's requirements
(described here:  http://www.student.uwa.edu.au/faculties/ems-students/exams/assessment )
was 65.9%,  which is with the required range for a Level-2 unit (65->70).

Of course, if you believe there's been a error in your result, there
are well advertised procedures to follow to have your result reviewed:

  http://www.governance.uwa.edu.au/committees/appeals-committee/review-and-appeal-of-academic-decisions-relating-to-students

Good luck,

Date: Sat Dec  1 18:05:41 2018
To: help2002@csse.uwa.edu.au

From: chris.mcdonald@uwa.edu.au

X-img: none

ANONYMOUS wrote:

> But I found no one between 50 and 60，and 
> lots of student failed.It was very 
> strange.I also found someone scale 20 mark, 
> someone just scale 1-5 mark,I don't think 
> it's fair

That's not quite true, but I can appreciate that the histogram gives that misleading 
view.

I've attached the distribution of overall marks.
You'll notice that there's a big 'bulge' of 21 students now sitting on exactly 50 and, 
clearly, that's the result of pushing a number of students to enable them to pass. 
You'll also find no marks on 59, 69, or 79, because students have been pushed to the 
next grade.

194 students passed, 114 failed of whom 79 sat but failed the final exam.
102 students passed, despite having failed the project; 34 students passed, despite 
having failed the exam; 19 failed both the project and the exam and still passed.

The overall distribution was not what I wanted, either at the beginning of semester, or 
now, but you cannot say that it's unfair (other than acknowledging that all approaches 
to scaling are unfair). Additive scale, where each mark is increased by the same amount, 
disproportionately rewards those with low marks; multiplicative scaling, where each mark 
is multiplied by the same amount, disproportionately rewards those with high marks; 
having to cap marks at 100 limits the scaling for those with high marks.

Whether you consider any adjustment to marks is fair probably depends on how it affects 
you. But, most importantly, no student's mark has gone down, no student has 'overtaken' 
another student that started with a higher mark, and any two students starting with the 
same mark finished with the same (increased) mark.

Of note, from next year no Faculties are permitted to apply any scaling; each marking 
rubric will provide the final marks for a project or exam, and the sum of those provides 
the final mark for the unit.  Everyone who currently believes scaling is an unfair 
system, will want to seriously think about their assessment from next year.

Date: Sun Dec  2 18:51:15 2018
To: help2002@csse.uwa.edu.au
Received: from 203.63.27.171
Subject: Unit result
From: chris.mcdonald@uwa.edu.au
X-smilie: none
X-img: none

ANONYMOUS wrote:

> So just confirming, next year there will 
> be no scaling? If the average is 40% 
> overall then that will be locked in as it 
> is?

Pretty much, though there's (always) some exceptions.
In general, unit coordinators and even departments cannot scale a unit's marks, either because they 'feel' 
scaling is necessary, or to meet any Faculty-wide rules that have required all units' averages to fall 
within specific ranges.  So, from Jan 1st, our Faculty's current policy:

  http://www.student.uwa.edu.au/faculties/ems-students/exams/assessment

no longer applies. This is a campus-wide change and a good thing because, if you're currently taking units 
from different faculties, some of your units may be scaled and others not.

The new Assessment Policy is defined here:

  http://www.governance.uwa.edu.au/procedures/policies/policies-and-procedures?method=document&id=up15/5

(the 'replacement' for scaling is in Section 9.2.3) and a summary of the changes is here:
  https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=7N6N4ztBmd4


R.I.P. curiosity and initiative.
